So if you are asking us for a plan to do this, let us start by adopting a properly discriminating attitude, and knowing exactly who has responsibility. And I say yes, let us discuss some things at European level. When you say, Mrs Moreau, that it is in the public interest to allow railway companies to work together, I say no. Indeed, it is by adding monopolies to monopolies that we have lost market share in freight transport. And that does not benefit the people. That has got nothing to do with public service, let me tell you. If there was a little more competition, we would be carrying much more on the railways than we do today. That is the reality. So if you are prepared to debate in this context the Commission will join you, but on condition that you speak the truth. I refuse to get into the old hat ideological debate some of you are having. Let that be clear. If you want the Commission to increase equality in services, I am perfectly willing to do so. If you were to give me more resource, the rules could be better applied with regard to the phenomenal increase in the number of cases requiring processing, and we could better serve companies and our customers. We are ready to do so. And I have, it seems to me, demonstrated that on many occasions in this Parliament. That is what I wanted to say to you. And to conclude, there are a number of amendments that the Commission can accept, and here I am referring to Amendment Nos 3, 6, 7 and 8. With regard to the latter, I would rather you did not restrict yourself to two decisions, but referred to all the decisions of the Court of Justice. I would also like to add Amendment Nos 10 and 13.

I have finished, Mr President, and I beg your pardon for having spoken for so long.

  President . – Thank you, Mr van Miert.

Mr Desama, the floor is yours.

  Desama (PSE). – (FR) Mr President, I have asked for the floor on a pointr of personal explanation, because I like a joke, but there are limits.

So thank you, Karel, for alluding to my position as a teacher, and it is precisely the teacher who is going to teach you a little lesson. The first thing is that before you criticize your teacher, you should first listen to what the teacher said. I never said that article 7d replaced article 90.2. At no time did I ever say that and it would be absurd to say so. So you should have listened to me more before you gave that reply.

The second lesson relates to the decision concerning monopoly on imports. I cited that decision because it is important for one thing, Mr Commissioner. This time, in its decision, the Court of Justice said that it is the Commission's responsibility to provide the proof that the monopoly is an impediment to free competition. And it is on that point that the decision of the Court of Justice is clearly an innovation.

  Donnelly, Alan (PSE). – Mr President, the important thing about this report is not just the amendments that the Commission will accept, but whether or not the Commissioner would sit down with our rapporteur early in the New Year and explore the ideas in the report to see what can be progressed between the European Commission and the European Parliament. In spite of the rhetoric that we have all been involved in this evening, there are some very concrete ideas in Mrs Billingham's report. What we would like to do is to progress some of those concrete ideas. If tonight you could give a commitment that you are willing to do that then certainly in the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy of the European Parliament we will try and take up those ideas and work with you to develop them.

  van Miert . – Obviously we are prepared to do so. But I think the right way to do it is to discuss the initiatives which the Commission develops in different sectors. Every sector has its own specifics and in light of the specifics of the sector we need to determine what kind of public service obligation – or whatever you call it – needs to be assured. This is how we have to work together. I certainly will be available to anyone – not just to the rapporteur – to make sure that we do not take risks as far as serving people everywhere in the European Union is concerned.

  President . – Thank you very much, Commissioner van Miert.

The debate is closed.

The vote will take place at 11.30 a.m. tomorrow.


14. Denominations in euros

  President . – The next item is the recommendation for second reading (A4‐0386/97) on behalf of the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy, concerning the common position established by the Council with a view to the adoption of a Council Regulation on denominations and technical specifications of euros coins intended for circulation (C4‐0598/97‐97/0154(SYN)) (rapporteur: Mrs Soltwedel‐Schäfer).

  Soltwedel‐Schäfer (V), rapporteur. – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, this will not be anywhere near as lively as Commissioner van Miert was when he gave his presentation. Allow me to comment that he was today the liveliest speaker of the whole part‐session. On the second reading of my report on denominations and technical specifications of euro coins, I would like to point out again briefly that at first reading the majority of Parliament wanted and voted for essential changes to three points. First, to reduce the number of coins from eight to six. Second, to create a relationship between the actual size and the form and shape. Third, to exclude nickel from all parts of the coin which come into contact with human skin. I would like to justify this again briefly.

We now have a total of eight coins, and in the whole EU and many nations of the EU there are at the moment six coins on average, sometimes even less. If we start from the fact that for many sections of the population, such as blind people for instance, it is very difficult to handle a large number of coins, this would be an example of a reason for reducing the total number of coins. Secondly, if we start from the fact that electronic money is constantly growing, this would be another reason.

The next argument, that the coin should grow with its value to avoid confusion of sizes, was also an important demand. For instance, the 5 cent coin, at 21.75 mm, is exactly the same size as the 20 cent coin, which makes things extremely difficult for people with disabilities.

The third issue is human health, an especially important aspect. There was also a majority on this point in Parliament at first reading. If we say that nickel should not to come into contact with human skin, we are only following other EU directives, which established this rule for other areas of daily need long ago.

The report was accepted in November with a large majority, but rejected by the Council. On 20 November, the Council adopted a common opinion on the proposed order. The European Parliament had decided by a large majority, but the Council only accepted Amendment Nos 8 and 12.

I was disappointed that the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy did not take advantage of its political opportunities in the last part‐session. The Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy practically anticipated the Council. There are now a few amendments, and incidentally I support all of them, but they are a long way from the point we were at during the November meeting.

What could we have done? Parliament could again adopt, by an absolute majority of its Members, all or some of the amendments which were rejected by the Council at first reading, as an amendment to the common position. We have this option according to the procedure under article 189c, and we could have done it. If the Commission or the Council show in the debate in the House that they are not prepared to accept Parliament's desired amendments to a sufficient extent, Parliament could again reject the common position, and that is what I want and have proposed. If the Council does not accept certain amendments, we can also break down the common position, and only accept it under conditions, that is, if the Council does not accept the amendments which Parliament has adopted, the common position is not approved.

Why do I repeat that again? I do not think we have not taken advantage of these opportunities in the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy. I would like to point out that the euro we will soon have, and which will certainly go down in European Union history as a successful and unique financial project, has the consequence that people will have the coins in their hands every day. Problems like confusion about size, like nickel, which is recognized to be a health problem, and many other points in my report will face the population every day, so the euro coin is important and these problems will come back to us as those who are politically responsible. I would therefore like to plead once again that we should take advantage of the political opportunities we have in Parliament.

  Wibe (PSE). – (SV) Mr President, as you know Sweden has decided to remain outside EMU. It is also my conviction that Sweden is going to remain outside monetary union for the foreseeable future. There is therefore no immediate Swedish interest in the design of the euro coin. However, I would still like to say a few words on the health aspects with regard to nickel in the outer layers of the coin.

10 % of all women and 1‐2 % of all men in Western Europe have developed a nickel allergy. These proportions are increasing, and 20 % of young women in Western Europe have this kind of illness. Nickel is the most common cause of contact allergies in the industrialized world.

A person who has become allergic to nickel keeps this allergy for the rest of their life. The allergy appears mainly in the form of eczema on the hands or on that part of the body which comes into contact with nickel. Cashiers, bank employees, waitresses, and so on, daily handle large numbers of coins, which are often made from nickel alloys. This coins deposit nickel on contact with the skin. It can also be shown that nickel is even passed by hand to nickelfree coins and notes. We have also proved that eczema on the hands of people allergic to nickel is made worse when they handle coins which contain nickel. Nickel in coins does not cause allergies, but makes the problem worse for people who have already developed this illness.

A decision to introduce coins containing nickel in the outer layers could prove very difficult to change and could cause serious health problems for a growing proportion of Europe's population, particularly for women, since they are often dominant in the professions where nickel coins are handled. For this reason at least the outer layer of the coins should be nickel‐free. In this case health reasons should take priority over economic considerations.

  Hoppenstedt (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, seeing the participation this evening, I can only say that the debate is obviously about small change. It seems pretty unimportant to all of us, but the scale of this small change is 70 billion coins, and for this reason we in the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy have taken it very seriously so far, and we have seriously tried to participate in the discussion about creating the new coins, by proposing amendments among other things.

The mint directors and finance ministers saw it otherwise. They have prepared texts, proposed strategies. But the Council did not accept what we discussed here, with quite small exceptions. My personal opinion is essentially that what we discussed here at first reading was right, and that what we decided was right. The breakdown of the coins, the increasing sizes, recognition by the blind and visually handicapped, also the old, traditional minting methods – all things which we have discussed and decided here – seem vitally important to me.

Last time we also defended the national side of the coins. That is now written into the Council's report, but we are very sad that our opinions were not accepted here. However, my opinion is that what Mrs Sollwedel‐Schäfer demanded here, in other words, the reintroduction of the earlier amendments, does not take us much further. The scenario is clear. In my opinion we should concentrate on two amendments, as we did in the economic committee, i.e. first the whole range of demands which have already been mentioned: increasing size, recognition of coins by blind people, recognition of coins by machines, protecting the larger coins from fraud. We have proposed this and got it through the economic committee. We should also decide on it in Parliament.

Secondly, we should pursue the matter of gold. We are not suffering from gold fever. People might have the idea that just before Christmas we caught gold fever, but we did not. We want there to be a 100 euro gold coin, which would continue old traditions and at the same time, as we have argued here, reduce Europe's gold reserves.

I think it is important to state again that protecting coins from forgery is very important to us, particularly as there are approximately 6 million coin‐operated machines in Europe. This involves the choice of alloys and recognition by machines. In this connection, let it be said that obviously not only the coins must be safe from forgery, but also all the notes. We have emphasized that many times. They must meet the highest security standards, so that they are also safe from forgery in future.

Let me plead once again for the amendments proposed by the economic committee and accepted there by a large majority to be accepted here in the House as well.

  Mezzaroma (UPE). – (IT) Mr President, it is not possible for Council to send us its decisions and for Parliament then to do nothing, even if it decided quite differently at first reading; it had decided to use metals that were not just nordic gold which is a metal primarily produced by certain states which will not be joining the euro. Moreover, the other choice of metal is just copper, and I wonder why. There are no plans to use stainless steel, which is a European metal; 20 % of world currency is made of stainless steel. This is an unacceptable situation. I wonder what the final solution will be. Finally, Council has relied on the fact that time is running out and no other decision can be taken, but that is certainly not true. The whole thing is still an insult to Parliament, which has expressed certain views and cannot just react to what the Council has decided.

  Peijs (PPE). – (NL) Mr President, what seemed to have become a purely technical report was suddenly given weight by the debate about the nickel in the coins and the national face. Parliament"s view on how euro coins should look was raised at first reading. I endorse the opinion of the rapporteur when she says that we should have fewer coins because the retail business will run into difficulties with a larger number of coins than we have now.

Unfortunately, the Council has dismissed the majority of the amendments and as long as the Council remains united it can do that. The fact that Parliament only has advisory powers in this area plays a part in this. The coin will become one of the most visible results of European integration, and respect would have been shown for the citizens if we had been able to influence this process through Parliament.

As a group we think nickel is acceptable in relation to combatting fraud. The Council denied this Parliament the right to submit an amendment on the national face because it was alleged to have nothing to do with technical specifications. As the Council knows very well, that is complete nonsense. To enable the Dutch to keep the edge inscription God zij met ons ‐God be with us – the edge has also been declared part of the national face. The edge and the national face therefore play a major part in the technical specifications which are needed to make the coins recognizable for the visually impaired, and for vending machines. Thus we believe that the national face is not just a graphic aspect, as the Council states, but most certainly a technical aspect, and the old Amendment No 28 therefore deserves a full place in the Council Resolution, and not merely in the recitals.

  Gallagher (UPE). – Mr President, I believe that the proposed regulation will send a further strong message that the euro is on the way and all EMU participants are playing their part. The aim of the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy's first amendment is to establish the principle of a single set of coins which would be designed so as to enable the visually impaired and the elderly, who have the greatest difficulty in distinguishing coins, to accept them more readily. The second amendment is a proposal to introduce a gold 100 euro coin for collectors. However the Treaty only provides for the harmonization of coins intended for circulation.

I am pleased that associations representing the visually impaired have been consulted on the euro coins. Furthermore, Recital 8 of the regulation underlines the importance of the relationship between the diameter and value of the coins, to which reference has been made. Our group believes that the coins, which will have one national and one common face, can be more easily recognized by those who use them by features such as their weight, shape, colour and diameter. In the case of most coins in use at the moment, the size of the coins is not directly linked to their face value. This can prove to be a difficulty for those who are using them.

As regards euro notes, we should not overlook the needs of the visually impaired and older people. We should ensure that for the visually impaired, in particular, the notes are distinguishable by touch. Perhaps there might be a response to that.

As regards the production of a 100 euro gold coin which for collectors might seem a very seductive proposition, it would be absolutely essential, if this proposal was taken up, to ensure that the cost of producing a gold coin did not exceed its face value, thereby imposing totally unacceptable costs on the Member States. On the other hand, could not the revenue from the sale of the 100 euro coins be transferred to the budget?

Taking account of these comments our group will be supporting this report.

  Rübig (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, the euro, and particularly the coin, is certainly a quintessential component of Europe. It shows on one side what we have in common, and on the other, individuality, as a symbol of Europe. I have seen the first designs in Austria, but I was somewhat surprised that “Euro’ is printed above the euro, but “Eurocent’ is printed above the cent. This could cause confusion. Also, the size of the coins is different, and for non‐experts it will really be quite difficult to distinguish the eight coins, instead of the six which have been normal up to now.

I will not dwell on the fact that many tills are equipped with six divisions, and must now be converted to eight. The eight coins will also result in enormous production costs, and given that electronic money will soon be introduced, we are faced with a mass of costs which could have been avoided.

I also wonder how the order will be placed. Will there be an invitation to tender with government orders? Will there be a competition? We have heard that we need 70 billion coins, so the costs will be enormous – how do these costs appear to citizens? Finally, I also believe that the 100 Euro gold coin can be a symbol of confidence in the new currency, even as a commemorative coin, even in a limited edition. I believe Europe has an important task here, and we must improve the coins a lot. Parliament made some proposals about this at first reading, but I believe it will be some time before we get the ideal system. This need for improvement must be flagged up today, and I hope that it will be carried out in the second round.

  de Silguy , Member of the Commission. – (FR) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, at this late hour I shall be brief, but first of all I want to tell you that the Commission is delighted that Parliament has been able to take a decision this evening on the second reading of the report by Mrs Soltwedel‐Schäfer on the technical characteristics of coins.

Indeed, this is an important question. The euro, and I say this often, is Europe in the pockets of our citizens. Consequently, coins are the way in which Europe takes on a real shape, as our citizens see it. This is the reason why the euro is not just a matter for the financial markets, it is a matter for the citizens, and it is for this reason that the regulation must be particularly carefully dealt with.

As Mr Hoppenstedt emphasized, there are now 70 billion coins in circulation. This means several tens of billions of coins to be minted between now and 1 January 2002. This will be done, Mr Rübig, by national administrations, in the name of the principle of subsidiarity, according to the procedures of invitations to tender, or of production which apply in each of the member countries.

I know that the formal decision of the Ministers of Finance can only be taken after 2 May, immediately after the decision of the Heads of State and of Government, but for technical and practical reasons which you will understand, in the light of the number of coins, you can appreciate how important it is to get this matter wrapped up at Council level, in terms of principles, in terms of a formal agreement, before the end of this year.

The proposal before you today at second reading is based on in‐depth work carried out for many months by the monetary authorities in the Member States and this has provided a guarantee of industrial feasibility, respecting the timetable agreed at Madrid in December 1995 by the Heads of State and of Government. I would add that this is the result of a long process of consultation, of concertation with all professional sectors concerned, with associations of consumers and with organizations representing the blind and visually impaired. The proposal made by the Commission is based on criteria of acceptability by public opinion – and we have tested that – on the basis of criteria of technical feasibility – and here we have assurances – and on public health criteria, because of course we do not want to take any risks with public health. This proposal is therefore a balanced one, and it meets both industrial constraints and the requirements of users.

Some of you, as was the case in the debate at first reading, criticized the number of coins. I wish to specify that the system is based on the experience acquired from all the Member States, and it ensures consistency with the unit values planned for notes. The number and series of coins have been defined as a response to the requirements of all consumers, taking into account the diversity of national situations currently existing. Today, in some Member States, the value of the smallest coin in circulation is around 1 euro cent.

Consensus has been reached on the size and shape of the coins, thanks, I would remind you, to the shape known as the “Latin flower’, which is not totally round, for the 20 cent piece. Consensus was also reached by defining three groups of coins, with three different colours, differentiating each coin within each group in terms of thickness and edging. Consequently, the preoccupations of the visually impaired have been very, very largely, I would say almost entirely, taken into consideration.

The technical specifications also represent high levels of protection against forgery, and this is particularly important for coins of high unit value, and here I am thinking of the 1 and 2 euro pieces. Finally, the technical specifications meet the concerns expressed in the area of public health, and provide the necessary assurances in that respect.

Here I want to say that the use of nickel in the Commission's proposal will be substantially reduced in Europe, as it will be limited to the 1 and 2 euro pieces, and that the volume of coins containing nickel will fall to 8 % of the total coins currently in circulation in Europe, that is, contractually by 75 %. I would add that the Toxicity and Ecotoxicity Committee, the scientific committee we approached on this proposal, supported this.

When the Ecofin Council adopted the common position last month, it took up the five amendments the Commission had accepted in its modified proposal. These relate, I would remind you, to the need for a reliable system, an effective system, and a system acceptable to all the citizens of the European Union.

Your rapporteur"s recommendation for second reading retables two amendments. As at first reading, the Commission cannot accept them. But let me specify the Commission"s position on one of Mrs Randzio‐Plath"s amendments, on the introduction of 100 euro gold coins. We have looked closely at this amendment, and from a legal point of view, the Commission cannot accept it within the framework of article 105a.2 which relates to euro denominations intended for circulation. Indeed, this proposal is in contradiction both to the introduction of the 100 euro note, which was already decided, and to the opposing view of central bankers, with regard to the circulation of such a coin.

However, in order to support the initiative, the Commission has suggested that all of the monetary authorities of the Member States should consider issuing a gold 100 euro collector's coin. This proposal should meet with the approval of the various Member States, and the Commission will keep you informed. I think that would be heavily symbolic. With the adoption of the regulation on the coins, the essential core of the arrangements required for moving to a single currency are now in place.

As it is late, I shall not go into the important decisions taken by the European Council at Luxembourg last week relating both to the coordination of economic policy, the euro Council, and the representation and definition of the external position of the euro in international bodies, but today we can say that all of the preparatory technical work relating to the euro is virtually complete. This evening, on this eve of the New Year, I would like to thank the European Parliament for its support throughout these months, for its work, for our collaboration, and for its commitment to a process leading up to the introduction of the euro, according to the timetable and the conditions laid down by the treaty.

The final technical preparations, which are now just details really, will be completed at the end of May 1998. The Commission is now working actively on this, and will naturally keep your Parliament strictly informed and closely associated with its efforts. The Commission will be bringing together – and I make this announcement this evening – a new round table on practical aspects of the euro, with all concerned partners, and of course with your Parliament. This round table will take place on 26 February in Brussels. The way is now open for the advent of the euro, and the political decisions will be taken on 2 May.

  President . – Thank you very much, Commissioner de Silguy.

The rapporteur, Mrs Soltwedel‐Schäfer, has a question.

  Soltwedel‐Schäfer (V), rapporteur. – (DE) Mr President, I know it is very late, but I would not be doing my job properly if I did not take up Mr Hoppenstedt's question. Perhaps Mr Hoppenstedt will stay here for a moment. First, I wanted to say to you, Mr de Silguy, that from my and our point of view cooperation and mutual information went very well. But I cannot understand the Commission and the Council not accepting these logically convincing amendments, which were accepted by a clear majority in Parliament and which really – as my colleague said – correspond to the interests of female citizens. I really cannot understand that.

But now to my question: Mr Hoppenstedt put a very important question about the minting of coins. Is there an invitation to tender, or is it like our showers in the Members' offices, another open door for corruption and dishonest enrichment? I would like to take up that question: is there an invitation to tender? Yes or no? I would like an answer this evening, one way or the other.

  de Silguy . – (FR) Mrs Soltwedel‐Schäfer, perhaps I expressed myself badly, but I thought I had replied to Mr Hoppenstedt on that point.

Every country must manufacture the stock of coinage required for its own market. Each country must also produce the stock of notes required for its own market. That is the principle adopted. So once we have the technical regulations, once we have the decision on the Community face of the coin, it will be the responsibility of every member country, in application of the principle of subsidiarity, firstly to define the graphics of the national face of the coin, and I think that a number of countries have already done so, and secondly to make the technical arrangements required for the production of the coins in question.

The same will apply to the manufacture of euro coinage as already occurs in the manufacture of national currency coins in Germany, France, the Netherlands and other countries. The manufacture will take place in accordance with the regulation applicable in each of the countries. In some countries these are virtually state‐run administrations, in other cases there are private companies and in yet others there are calls for competitive tender. This is a question which is the responsibility of each of the Member States. Manufacture will take place in the same conditions of legality and regularity and as currently apply in the production of national coinage.

  Rübig (PPE). – (DE) I only wanted to ask, must these states then issue invitations to tender for eight coins, or would six be enough?

  de Silguy . – (FR) For eight coins, because the intention is to circulate eight coins in all of the Member States.

  President . – Thank you very much, Commissioner de Silguy.

The debate is closed.

The vote will take place at 11.30 a.m. tomorrow.(1)

(The sitting was closed at 12.11 a.m.)

ANNEX

Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 37 by Sören Wibe (H−0867/97)

Subject: Future implementation of Council Directive 96/96/EC

I have carried out a study which clearly shows that there are currently wide variations in Member States' roadworthiness tests for motor vehicles and trailers. Council Directive 96/96/EC of 20 December 1996(1) aims to approximate the laws of the Member States in this area. Sweden has complied but nothing much seems to have happened in many other Member States.

Does the Commission have a clear view of the situation ahead of harmonization in 1998? Will the Member States implement Directive 96/96/EC during 1998?

Answer

The directive on roadworthiness testing does not harmonize the level of standards of inspection but rather sets the minimum standard that must be followed by Member States. Interpretation of the Directive with regard to:

‐ the severity and frequency of testing and

‐ the authorization and control of test centres;

is left to Member States.

The Commission recognizes with regret that the quality and rigour of the roadworthiness programmes varies between and across Member States. Within the terms of the current law, it is the Commission's task to try to enhance the roadworthiness requirements when justifiable on cost effectiveness grounds, and in the long term that process will bring about a convergence of the national standards.

The Commission also believes that the introduction of kerbside checks made without warning could prove to be an effective deterrent against poor maintenance and consequently it is developing a proposal for the introduction of random inspections of the roadworthiness condition of vehicles.

With regard to implementing the directive, the Commission will, as always, monitor the transposal into national legislation.

***

Question No 38 by Mark Watts (H−0877/97)

Subject: Passenger registration on cross‐channel services

Will the Commission please say what progress has been made on the proposal to extend passenger registration provisions to le Shuttle services in undersea tunnels over 20 miles in length, given that the proposal was supported by Parliament at first reading?

Answer

The honourable Member will recall that, at the first reading of this proposal, the Commission did not accept the suggestion that ferry passenger registration should be extended to cover le Shuttle services. It did, however, agree to investigate the merits of such a proposal and that assessment is continuing.

In the course of it, attention is focused on two questions:

‐ whether the Channel Tunnel (which is obviously a modern twin bore tunnel with electronic surveillance equipment and a prohibition on the carriage of petroleum, chemicals, nuclear waste, etc.) should be the subject of further special rules which do not apply to other long rail tunnels, and

‐ whether there is justification in European Community legislation rather than statutory provision by the relevant Member States.

The Commission is finalizing its consideration of the matter and will formally report to the House during the second reading of the proposal.

***

Question No 39 by Gerhard Schmid (H−0897/97)

Subject: Deregulation of local public transport

Is it true that in mid−1998 the Commission will be proposing legislation seeking further deregulation of local public transport in the Member States, and if so, why is this not mentioned in the Commission's programme of work for 1998?

Answer

As stated in the 1995 Citizens' Network Green Paper(2) and the 1996 Railways White Paper(3) , the Commission is examining in areas in which current legal arrangements for domestic passenger transport might need to be updated.

The Commission's objective in undertaking that review is to encourage better transport provision, while ensuring that rights to operate services are awarded in ways that are compatible with Community competition law.

The Commission has already commissioned an in‐depth study of the current application of regulation 1191/69 on Public Service Obligations, and an expert group of Member State representatives has been set up to act as a sounding board for the study.

The Commission would draw the honourable Member's attention to the fact that the Commission announced in its programme of new legislation for 1998 its intention to bring forward legislative proposals during next year.

These proposals are intended:

First, to require public service obligations to be set out clearly in contracts, and

Second, to safeguard transparency, neutrality and objectivity by introducing an element of competition in the award of public service contracts and of exclusive contracts or rights.

The proposals will, of course, respect the principle of subsidiarity, and the fundamental decisions about the nature of the transport services to be provided will continue to be a matter for determination by the competent authorities within Member States.

The Commission believes, in particular, that public authorities in the Member States should retain powers to ensure that transport services are adequate in terms of quality, quantity and price, and that transport services and systems are integrated.

The system of tender for contracts would ensure competition for the service rather than in the service, whilst the contracts themselves would specify the standards of service that the operator would be obliged to maintain and the level of support which authorities would be obliged to provide.

The whole motive of such proposals will be to strengthen transport service quality in order to serve consumers and to increase the attractiveness of public passenger transport.

***

Question No 40 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0900/97)

Subject: Community shipping

The European Court of Justice has recently ruled that henceforth even shipowners who are not Greek nationals may operate domestic maritime lines, providing they are entered on the Greek shipping register. Bearing in mind the principles of non‐discrimination, freedom of establishment and freedom of movement of persons invoked by the European Court of Justice, will the Commission say whether it has addressed – or how it intends to address – the subject of safety and customer service and how it would view a proposal making it compulsory for crews of vessels to be familiar with the Greek language, so as to prevent communication problems in an emergency, for instance?

Answer

The Commission is aware that after the recent Court of Justice decision, Greek legislation on the registration of vessels will have to be amended so that shipowners who do not have Greek nationality are able to operate in national passenger services by means of registering a vessel in the Greek register. They will still, of course, have to comply with the relevant national and international safety legislation.

In accordance with Regulation 3577/92 which applies the cabotage freedom to provide services to maritime transport within Member States, regular passenger services in Greek mainland trade will be open to Community shipowners, as defined in the Regulation, who are operating vessels registered in and flying the flag of another Member State, from 1 January1999, and in the island trade from 1 January 2004.

On the issue of on‐board communication, Directive 94/58 on the minimum level of training of seafarers, conforms with the relevant IMO regulations and provides that, on board passenger ships, personnel nominated on muster lists to assist passengers in emergency situations must be able to speak the language appropriate to the principal nationality of passengers. In the area which concerns the honourable Member, that would obviously mean the Greek language.

Given that there is no international or Community legislation defining the exact number and functions of crew members to be stated on muster lists, it is up to national authorities – in line with the relevant IMO Safety of Life at Sea guidelines – to determine the requirements by taking into account factors like size of the ship, number of passengers, sea conditions typical on the route and duration of voyages.

***

Question No 41 by Jörn Svensson (H−0908/97)

Subject: Guidelines for reducing road traffic

The Commission Green Paper on the impact of transport on the environment rightly emphasizes the high indirect cost of road traffic. At the same time the long term trend is an increase in the proportion of traffic carried by road. This is encouraged by industry's “lean production’ system, the aim of which is to keep inventory to a minimum.

Will the Commission submit guidelines for halting this trend and encouraging the transfer of traffic to rail and waterway?

Answer

As the honourable Member will know, sustainable mobility is at the heart of the common transport policy and, as part of that policy, the Commission works to secure a move away from dependence on road transport towards more environmentally‐friendly modes, such as rail and waterways. Since 1995 in particular, several Commission initiatives have been produced to promote that modal shift and they include:

‐ efforts to foster short sea shipping,

‐ a strategy for the revitalization of the railways, and the establishment of rail freight freeways,

‐ efforts to support freight intermodality,

‐ the development of a citizens" network approach to strengthen development of public passenger transport,

‐ and a Green Paper and consultative activity on fair and efficient transport pricing.

In addition, the transport research programme includes several projects related to sustainability.

The Commission is, naturally, well aware of the environmental problems caused by road transport, and will continue to urge Member States and all involved in transport operations to implement the strategies outlined in the various Commission texts.

The Commission is, meanwhile, acutely aware of the fact that if the current growth rates in road transport continue, conditions will become increasingly congested, dangerous and costly, especially in urban areas and on important transport corridors, and whilst modal shift alone may not be able to halt this trend it can certainly ease current and developing pressures. The Commission will be examining this issue, amongst others, when it presents a communication on CO2 emissions from transport next year.

***

Question No 42 by Jan Andersson (H−0927/97)

Subject: Development of rail traffic in central regions of the European Union

A combined rail and road link is now being built between Sweden and Denmark. This link will not be able to improve the competitiveness of rail with road transport as there is no fixed link across the Fehmarn Belt, and because of the inadequacy of the rail links in the northern part of Germany.

What action does the Commission intend to take to improve rail links from the central regions of the European Union to the countries of Scandinavia?

Answer

The Community is responsible for the identification of guidelines covering general objectives, priorities and the broad lines of measures for the development of the trans‐European networks. The outline plan for rail and road included in the decision(4) on the development of the TEN guidelines shows the Fehmarn Belt as a project of common interest to be realized by 2010. Improvements in rail infrastructure connecting Germany to Denmark are also included in the guidelines, in particular the planned upgrading for high speed of the line north of Hamburg to Puttgarden. However, it must be recalled that decisions on particular projects remain a matter for the Member States.

The Commission is aware of the strategic importance of a possible link across the Fehmarn Belt, and has contributed financially to investigations undertaken by the Danish and German authorities. Community intervention has also assisted the Danish authorities in upgrading rail connections from Öresund to Germany via the Great Belt route, notably through track‐doubling and electrification work between Odense and Padborg.

Regarding rail connections between northern Scandinavia and the Öresund, it should be noted that the Commission has supported a number of major upgrading projects on the Swedish west coast line (Malmö‐Göteborg) and on the southern main line (Stockholm‐Malmö).

In addition – and apart from support for developments in physical infrastructure – the Commission is active in the promotion of rail freight freeways and other organizational and technical advances which can improve the quality of services in ways that increase the commercial attractiveness of rail and enhance the sustainable use of existing links.

Question No 45 by Ulf Holm (H−0885/97)

Subject: Proposed project for the completion of Khmelnitsky 2 and Rovno 4 nuclear reactors (K2/R4) in the Ukraine

The proposed completion of K2/R4 forms part of the Memorandum of Understanding between G7 and the Ukraine on the Closure of Chernobyl (MOU), but only on the basis of least cost planning principles as specified in Title II. Can the Commission state the extent of the budgetary implications associated with the Euratom Loan currently under consideration for K2/R4, via the Guarantee Fund; bearing in mind these implications, does the Commission find it correct that procedures have deviated from those clearly established in the MOU, in that the results of the only valid least cost study made under the MOU are being ignored, and a new unofficial one has been commissioned outside the ambit of MOU for the purpose of trying to get an approval? Does not the fact that the project is clearly far removed from least cost make any such loan a very risky one, thereby compromising the EU budget?

Answer

As far as the economic aspect of the proposed loan is concerned, the Commission will consider all available elements. The study performed by the international panel chaired by Professor Surrey is one of such elements.

With regard to the extent of the budgetary implications of the Euratom loan for the K2/R4 project, the rules for Community external financing will apply. However, a more precise answer can only be given once the exact amount of this loan is fixed. At present, the relative share of the different lenders is still under consideration.

Question No 46 by Hiltrud Breyer (H−0833/97)

Subject: Proposed Euratom loan for Kozloduy nuclear reactors 5 & 6 in Bulgaria and the closure of units 1 to 4

On 27 October 1997, the Council of Ministers of Bulgaria adopted a Memorandum for the extended closure of Kozloduy nuclear reactor units 1‐2 after 2005 and units 3‐4 after 2010; this would break the Nuclear Safety Account (NSA) Agreement, which sought the closure of all 4 units by 2000; since the Commission is a contributor to the NSA, can the Commission confirm that should the Bulgarian authorities carry out the full implications of this Memorandum of Understanding, that the Euratom loan for units 5 and 6, presently under negotiations would be withdrawn?

Answer

The Commission has taken note of the Memorandum in question and in particular of the fact that the Bulgarian Government would like to review the terms of the NSA Grant Agreement of 16 June1993 (paragraph 3.07: Energy Sector Development and Shutdown of Certain Units of Kozloduy NPP).

As one of the contributors to the NSA, the Commission will participate in the preparation of the response to be given by the EBRD to the Bulgarian Government on this subject.

At this stage in the negotiations between Euratom and the Bulgarian Electricity Company (NEK) concerning a possible loan for the modernization of Kozloduy units 5 and 6, the Commission intends to include conformity with the NSO agreement as a condition in the Euratom loan agreement.

It is expected that by the time a final decision on the loan is taken, the proposal of the Bulgarian Government will have been fully examined and the NSA position with respect to the NSA Grant Agreement will have been determined.

At this stage, it should be noted that there is as yet no agreement to grant a Euratom loan and therefore no question of withdrawing it.

Question No 47 by Bernd Posselt (H−0891/97)

Subject: EU‐Croatia agreement

Why is there still no EU‐Croatia agreement, and what are the Commission's aims and timetable for 1998 in this area?

Answer

A decision was taken in March 1995 to extend Phare assistance to Croatia, and to open negotiations on a trade and cooperation agreement. The assistance and the negotiations were, however, suspended on 4 August 1995 following Croatian military offensives in the Krajina.

In April 1997, the Council set out coherent and transparent conditions relating to Phare assistance and the opening of negotiations on a cooperation agreement with the countries covered by the regional approach with which we do not yet have association agreements, that is, Croatia and its neighbours, Bosnia‐Herzogovina and the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia. Application of conditionality is considered essential for achieving the EU's foreign policy objectives, namely stability and peace in the area of the former Yugoslavia.

While some progress has been observed, Croatia has clearly not yet made sufficient progress in compliance with these conditions to permit the opening of negotiations on a cooperation agreement. This was confirmed by the General Affairs Council of 10 November.

Question No 48 by Maj Theorin (H−0899/97)

Subject: Situation in Belarus

The situation in Belarus is disastrous. Democracy is not just threatened, it has ceased to exist. Demonstrations are banned and parliament has been dissolved. The President has appointed his own parliament. Civil society (including aid to the children of Chernobyl) and the free press have been crushed. This week the country's largest independent newspaper, Svaboda, was closed by presidential decree on the grounds that its activity was regarded as hostile to the government.

Last spring Belarus and Russia formed a union. Belarus is strategically important to Russia as a country on its western border and as a symbol that the Soviet Union has not only fragmented but has also to some extent been restored. Does the Commissioner believe that it would be possible for the EU to make demands on Belarus through its relations with Russia?

What is the Commission doing to support the people of Belarus in their struggle for human rights and democracy in a country with an ever more dictatorial leader?

Answer

The Commission closely follows the deterioration of the internal situation in Belarus with utmost concern. (Belarus is a European country.) In line with the Council Conclusions of 15 September 1997, and to avoid further isolation of the country, the Commission has proposed to the Council to set up an ad hoc ECU 5 million 1997 Tacis Programme for the development of civil society in Belarus, based on the human rights clause in the Tacis Regulation. The programme would benefit the free media, the NGOs as well as the education sector, three pillars for the development of democracy in Belarus, and would complement substantially to the democracy programme which continues to operate in Belarus. The Council Decision is expected by mid‐December.

The Commission continues to provide humanitarian aid to Belarus, notably in relation to public health, especially concerning children, in the aftermath of the Chernobyl accident. On 9 September 1997 it approved another ECU 1 million grant in aid for the victims of Chernobyl, the children in particular.

A number of regional and local initiatives are supported by the Commission through Tacis regional programmes, with the aim of developing, at local and regional level, democratic institutions and practices.

The Commission is conscious of the leverage that Russia has over Belarus. It has repeatedly pleaded for a strong Russian stance towards the Belarus regime, consistent with the signature of the partnership and cooperation agreement and its Council of Europe membership. However, Belarus also seems to have a certain leverage over Russia. Russian political spheres are divided over Belarus and Russia needs Belarus politically and geostrategically. So far, Russia has made little use of its influence and does not seem to be ready to denounce its neighbour publicly.

***

Question No 49 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0914/97)

Subject: The situation in Belarus

Belarus is sliding towards increasingly hard‐line dictatorship. The democratically elected parliament has been dissolved, journalists are imprisoned, TV channels are banned, members of the public are convicted by presidential decree, demonstrators are arrested, and there have been several reports from the Belarus opposition concerning the brutal ill‐treatment of people held in prison or detained in custody. Russia and Belarus are two sovereign states, despite the union between them. Is there any common supranational authority linking them and, if so, can the Commission bring pressure to bear on President Lukashenka via such an authority?

Answer

On 23 May 1997, Russia and Belarus signed a Union Charter. The Charter is part of the Union Treaty signed on 2 April 1997. The Charter was ratified by both the Russian and Belarus Parliaments. The Union is a subject of international law. The Charter provides for the creation of three Union bodies, the Higher Council, the Parliamentary Assembly and the Executive Committee.

The European Commission would not consider the Higher Council, the highest Union body, as a supranational entity. Under the Union Charter, Russia and Belarus maintain their full sovereignty within the Union, contrary to the principle of supranationality. The decisions of the Higher Council, which actually look more like recommendations, are not binding. They need to be validated at national level by the Governments of the Member States.

In view of the intergovernmental character of the Higher Council of which President Lukashenko himself is a member, the Commission cannot expect this Council to be willing to exert pressure on President Lukashenko.

The European Commission, however, believes in the leverage of Russia over Belarus. It has therefore repeatedly, and at all levels, called for a meaningful role of Russia in the appeasement of the internal situation in Belarus, based on its Council of Europe membership and the signature and recent entry into force of the partnership and cooperation agreement. Russia does not share the position of the EU towards Belarus, which links, in particular, the renewal of bilateral relations with the restoration of the status quo before the November 1996 referendum. Russia has not opposed the decision of the OSCE to establish an OSCE monitoring and advisory group in Minsk, but has rather taken up the defence of Belarus in its difficult negotiations for the establishment of this group.

Question No 50 by Astrid Thors (H−0915/97)

Subject: Children's rights in the countries applying for membership in the EU

In its evaluation of the applicant countries, children and their rights are only mentioned in the reports on Romania, Bulgaria and Lithuania. Only in the report on Romania is there reference to its obligations under the UN Convention on the Rights of the Child and the amount of Phare assistance used in the country to improve children's conditions.

Is the Commission aware of whether the applicant countries have fulfilled their obligations according to the UN Convention on the Rights of the Child; is the Commission ready to include monitoring of this convention in its annual assessment of applicant countries and is the Commission ready to include in the Phare reports how much money is spent to improve children's conditions?

Answer

First, in the Commission"s opinions on the applications from central and eastern European countries, it looked carefully at the issue of respect for children"s rights and reported any violation of these rights of which it was aware. So it quoted the current situation in the three countries referred to by the honourable Member and also the position regarding adolescents in Latvian prisons.

Secondly, the Commission will continue to follow the situation in all the applicant countries closely, in particular through its annual reports on the progress of preparations for accession in these countries.

Thirdly, the Commission is also prepared to include in the annual Phare reports the sum allowed for in the national programmes for the improvement of the situation of children in these countries.

Question No 51 by Gary Titley (H−0917/97)

Subject: NATO presence and the peace process in Bosnia

What in the Commission's view would be the impact on the peace process in Bosnia of the failure to agree a new NATO presence after the mandate for SFOR expires in June 1998?

Answer

The Peace Implementation Council meeting in Bonn on 9/10 December concluded that ‚the single greatest contribution to sub‐regional security in the short to medium term would be the continued engagement of a NATO‐led military force providing a secure environment to civilian implementation in Bosnia and Herzegovina post June 1998’(5) . The Commission fully endorses this judgment and has been grateful for the close collaboration between SFOR and the civilian implementation agencies. Without the secure environment currently provided by SFOR, the work of the High Representative and all those working to implement the peace agreement would be immeasurably harder. So the Commission welcomes the fact that NATO Ministers have already set in hand the process of planning for what sort of force might be needed after the end of SFOR's mandate.

Question No 52 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0924/97)

Subject: Bomb attack and Turkish restrictions on the Patriarchate of Constantinople

The Patriarchate of Constantinople has been the repeated target of terrorist attacks. The recent bomb attack caused much damage and one deacon was seriously injured. These tragic events are related to attempts to undermine and put political pressure on the Patriarchate by the Turkish Government which is fomenting religious intolerance by refusing to allow the reopening of the Chalki Theological College and making it compulsory for the Patriarch and office‐holders at the Patriarchate to be Turkish citizens. All the above circumstances, added to the mass persecution of the Orthodox population of Turkey, continue to foment religious intolerance and also weaken and gradually undermine this institution.

Will the Commission say what measures it will take to put pressure on Turkey to take immediate action to protect the Oecumenical Patriarchate, to reopen the Chalki Theological College and to lift the oppressive provisions making it compulsory for office‐holders of the Oecumenical Patriarchate to be Turkish nationals?

Answer

The Commission deplores the recent attacks on the Patriarchate of Constantinople. Responsibility for the most recent attack has been claimed by an Islamic movement. The Turkish authorities have condemned this attack and security has been tightened around the Patriarchate.

Furthermore, the Turkish authorities have informed the Commission that the obligation to become a Turkish citizen to which the honourable Member refers applies only to the Patriarchate itself and not to the other members of the Patriarchate. This obligation follows from a specific clause in the Lausanne Treaty.

As regards the re‐opening of the Chalki Theological College, the Turkish constitution does not permit the operation of private academic institutions which provide religious teaching. However, the Turkish authorities recently indicated that they were at present actively considering re‐opening this college under the supervision of the Turkish Minister of Education.

Question No 53 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0929/97)

Subject: Structural funds support 2000‐2006 for countries applying for EU membership

In its financial perspective concerning enlargement, the Commission has proposed allocating funds totalling ECU 74.8 billion to support the applicant countries up until the year 2006. Under heading 2 (structural operations), it is proposed to allocate a total of ECU 45 billion to the applicant countries, with ECU 38 billion going to new Member States and only ECU 7 billion in the form of pre‐accession aid to those countries which do not qualify for first‐round membership. This enormous difference in treatment is also evident in relation to the overall allocation of support.

Does the Commission agree that all the applicant countries face the same types of problem which need to be resolved before or as they join the EU and that, to start with, they all have the same need for financial support?

Does the Commission not agree that, by favouring one group of countries over the other, the EU risks widening the gulf between the two of them?

Will the Commission modify its proposals so that the applicant countries which do not qualify for membership in the first round receive substantially more financial support than it has so far proposed and so that greater account is taken of those countries' needs and the desire to prevent a widening of the economic and social disparities that exist between the groups of countries?

Answer

In its communication, Agenda 2000, the Commission proposes a sum of ECU 21 billion for pre‐accession support during the period 2000‐2006, that is, ECU 3 billion per year. This support is made up of ECU 1.5 billion from Phare, ECU 1 billion under heading 2 and ECU 500 million under heading 1. The different interventions will be coordinated and allocated to all the applicant countries according to their specific needs.

As soon as a country joins the Community the new Member State takes on all the rights and obligations of membership, including access to the structural funds, CAP and funding under other Community policies; it will of course no longer receive pre‐accession assistance.

The Commission drew up its financial proposals on the basis of a technical hypothesis of five CEECs and Cyprus joining in 2002. According to this hypothesis, therefore, after 2002 the annual sum of ECU 3 billion of pre‐accession assistance will be divided among only five candidate countries, each of which will receive a correspondingly larger share of this money.

The Commission"s financial proposals seem substantial and well‐balanced. In fact, compared with the level planned for the candidate countries in 1999, pre‐accession aid represents more than double. Moreover, as a percentage of beneficiary GDP, the aid granted, on the one hand to the new Member States and on the other to the remaining candidate countries, is still comparable over the whole period (2.6 % on average for the former and 3.2 % for the latter) according to our hypothesis.

If enlargement was going to take place under substantially different conditions, the amounts earmarked by the Commission for pre‐accession aid and allocations to new members could be reviewed.

Subject: Re‐examination of anti‐dumping measures relating to imports of potassium chloride from CIS countries and the threat to employment

Its conclusions will be crucial for the future of the European potash market. If the price of potash were to fall, large numbers of jobs would be seriously affected in the near future (the Upper Rhine potash‐mining area, for instance, which is already affected by the decision to close plant in 2004, would thus be faced with premature closure).

One of the EU's aims is supposed to be to create greater openness, transparency and democratic control in its operations, which means that proposals for legislation must be as easily comprehensible as possible. This is far from always the case.

The Commission does not share the view of the honourable Member of Parliament that the Commission proposal for a European Parliament and Council Directive amending European Parliament and Council Directive 95/2/EC on food additives other than colours and sweeteners (COD 96/1066) is incomprehensible. Question No 57 by Kirsi Piha (H−0862/97)

Subject: European Union export subsidies for animal transport

During its last part‐session in Strasbourg (24 October 1997), Parliament considered the excellent report by Sirkka‐Liisa Anttila(7) concerning transportation of animals within the EU and to third countries, an issue which has aroused much public concern. In the debate, many Members criticized the very payment of export subsidies in general. What will the Commission do to reduce to a minimum the transportation of live animals, often over long distances, which is at odds with the principle of respect for the Union's animals? Will the Commission put before Parliament a proposal for the abolition of all export subsidies?

Answer

In 1995 exports of live animals amounted to 16 % of total beef exports and in 1996 to 13 %. For 1997 an estimate based on licence applications indicates that live exports represent 7.8 % of total beef exports. This figure indicates a further reduction of exports of live animals. Nevertheless, export of live animals is an important outlet for the Community beef market and can hardly be substituted by export of beef meat due to the different slaughtering rites, the lack of coldstores in importing countries or the specific demand for fresh meat of the required quality in these countries. It must be recognized as a matter of fact that if the export of live animals from the Community were to become impossible due to the phasing out of export refunds, other suppliers would take over these markets. Australia increasingly exports live animals to the Arab world with a journey considerably longer than from the Community.

The proposal debated in the European Parliament during the October plenary session, for which Mrs Antilla was rapporteur, stipulates that in the case of exports of live animals, the payment of the export refund shall be subject to compliance with the provisions established in Community legislation concerning animals welfare and, in particular, the protection of animals during transport. Parliament gave a generally supportive opinion on the Commission proposal for this draft regulation. On that occasion an amendment calling for the abolition of the export refunds for live animals was rejected by a large majority.

The Commission does not intend to put before the Parliament a proposal for the abolition of export subsidies for live animals. However, this Regulation will considerably increase the protection of animals during transport.

Question No 58 by Patricia McKenna (H−0827/97)

Subject: Exports of live animals from Ireland to France

A new service for exporting live animals from Cork, Ireland to Cherbourg, France began recently. Live exports from Ireland to the continent had been severely reduced over the preceding two years, as several shipping companies refused to carry live animals following protests by the animal welfare movement.

EU Council Directive 91/628/EEC(8) (as amended by Council Directive 95/29/EC(9) stipulates that provision be made for adequate resting periods for animals who are transported live.

Has the Commission investigated whether or not this directive is being complied with in the case of live exports from Ireland to France? In particular, is it satisfied that arrangements are being made to ensure that animals arriving in Cherbourg are rested for 12 hours before going further?

If the Commission has not yet investigated this matter, will it do so promptly?

Answer

The European Commission is aware that transport of live cattle is taking place from Ireland to France, via a rollon/roll‐off ferry, but has not monitored these operations in 1997. The Commission is thus not in a position to answer the question raised in relation to the application of Council Directive 91/628/EEC on the protection of animals during transport, in this particular case. The responsibility lies with the Irish and French authorities.

During a mission carried out in 1995, a Commission official checking sea transport conditions in two Irish ports came to the conclusion that the Irish authorities have made a great effort to avoid ill treatment and losses of cattle during sea transport.

This is the reason why the Commission is now concentrating its limited resources to monitor sea transport livestock in other areas of the Community where deficiencies were found in the past.

A mission to Ireland may, nevertheless, take place during the forthcoming months.

Question No 59 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0864/97)

Subject: The associated countries of the Maghreb and the European voluntary service for young people (OJ C 302 of 3.10.97)

The programme is intended to step up cooperation with third countries with which the Community has signed association agreements, and as regards the participation of the associated countries, it is open to the countries of central and eastern europe; one of the purposes of the project is to provide Community young people with experience of contacts with other cultures in an intercultural context.

Could the Commission investigate the possibility of opening up participation in this programme to the partner countries of the Maghreb which have signed a Euro Mediterranean Association Agreement, i.e. Tunisia and Morocco? How soon could this be done?

This includes voluntary service activities in the countries which have signed a Euro‐Mediterranean Association agreement. Question No 60 by Gerard Collins (H−0866/97)

Subject: Farm retirement scheme and land prices

The farm retirement scheme is playing a valuable role in changing the age profile of Europe's farmers. However, the requirement that the size of farms must be increased is artificially inflating the price of small parcels of land to the extent that, in many areas, prices are at uneconomic levels. Has the Commission examined the impact of this requirement to increase the size of holdings on land prices and will it now put forward proposals to eliminate this requirement from the farm retirement scheme?

Answer

The requirement to increase the size of the holding by the transferee is one of the essential mandatory conditions for granting aids to transferors in the framework of the Community early retirement scheme. This condition has been established in order to guarantee the objective of this mainly structural measure, that is to improve the viability of transferred agricultural holdings. Concerning the impact of this enlargement clause on land prices, neither is there information available on Community level nor has the attention of the Commission been drawn to the problem of increasing land prices due to this condition by Member States. The abolition of the enlargement clause is not envisaged for the moment, without prejudice to the outcome of current reflection on the future of Community measures.

Question No 61 by José Valverde López (H−0869/97)

Subject: Euro‐Mediterranean Association Agreement with Morocco

The Euro‐Mediterranean Association Agreement with Morocco increased the concessions granted by the European Union in the fruit and vegetables sector, Andalusia being the Community region most affected by this.

What is the Commission's assessment of the impact of imports from Morocco on the Andalusian fruit and vegetable sector?

Answer

The new EC‐Morocco agreement which was approved by the Council on 26 February 1996 has not yet been ratified by all the national parliaments of the Member States and therefore has not yet entered into force. The concessions which are in force refer back to those of the 1978 cooperation agreement. Since 1 January 1996, by way of anticipation, only a limited number of Moroccan products have benefitted from concessions under the new agreement, which were negotiated within the framework of the WTO. This anticipation aims to maintain certain traditional Moroccan exports affected by the new trade conditions established in the Uruguay Round agreement, and affects tomatoes, aubergines, courgettes, oranges and clementines.

However, in the new agreement, new mechanisms ensure the protection of European production. Since coming into force they have already proved very efficient (weekly monitoring and quantity ceilings at agreed levels for Moroccan exports, quotas, marketing schedules, application of contractual entry prices, etc.).

These mechanisms prevent damage to production within the Community, including Andalusia, and have not stopped the expansion of production; this can be observed by the production of tomatoes and citrus fruits in Spain which is constantly on the increase. It should be noted that the Community has made an internal decision on a very sensitive product for Andalusia, cut flowers, to introduce promotional measures to stimulate the marketing of flowers inside the Community.

Finally, the new agreement also contains concessions for exports of European agricultural products to Morocco. These concessions should particularly benefit certain Andalusian products, all the more so as the proximity of Morocco represents a trading advantage.

Question No 62 by Per Gahrton (H−0870/97)

Subject: Ban on antibiotics

In the light of the Swedish Government's representations to the Commission to submit a proposal for a directive banning antibiotics, along the lines of the current Swedish ban, and to allow Sweden to retain its exemption once the period for exemptions expires on 31 December 1998, will the Commission say whether it is preparing a proposal for a directive banning antibiotics? What will the Commission propose in Sweden's case if there is no general EU ban on antibiotics by 31 December 1998?

Answer

The Commission recognizes that the Swedish government has recently been intensifying initiatives geared to obtaining a general Community ban on antibiotics in animal fodder.

However these efforts will be in vain if Sweden does not provide the scientific evidence, as undertaken at its accession, to convince the Commission and the Member States of the good grounds for a ban of this kind.

Legally, the Commission cannot actually call a ban without having serious health reasons for doing so. If it did otherwise, it would inevitably open itself up to severe criticism both from Member States and from the World Trade Organization.

By delaying clarifying its request for a ban, Sweden is running the risk that the Commission will not be in a position to rule on this in time and of then being obliged to lift the current ban on using antibiotics within its own borders.

However, the Commission can assure the honourable Member that as soon as it receives the necessary scientific justification, this will be examined with the the greatest seriousness and all the necessary speed with a view to legislating before time runs out on 31 December 1998.

Question No 63 by Karl Habsburg‐Lothringen (H−0874/97)

Subject: Human rights in Nagaland

The peace process for Nagaland has made some progress in the last few weeks and months. The ceasefire agreed on 1 August 1997 between the Indian army and the NSCN is currently holding. But experience of recent years has shown that agreements that are supposed to lead to greater autonomy for Nagaland have repeatedly been broken, especially on the Indian side.

What does the Commission propose to do to ensure that the Indian Government is urged to direct this conflict, which has been escalating for 43 years, towards a settlement in terms of democracy and the right of peoples to self‐determination?

Answer

Following the discussions in Geneva in 1996, the Indian government concluded a cease‐fire agreement with the Isak‐Muivah faction of the National Socialist Council of Nagaland (the main guerrilla faction) on 1 August 1997. The Commission is delighted that this agreement has been reached. Since then, the Indian government has unilaterally extended this armistice to the other rebels and has withdrawn its armed forces at numerous strategic points. The Commission is kept informed of developments on the ground by means of its delegation in New Delhi.

As the honourable Member pointed out, the ceasefire has been observed up to now, and at the moment the Commission does not have any indication which would lead it to believe that the Indian government will not honour its undertakings in the agreement of 1 August. Be that as it may, the recent clashes seem to have been mainly between the different Naga factions which are fighting to establish their supremacy.

Naturally, the Commission is keen to encourage constructive dialogue in order to reach a lasting peace. If it proved necessary, the Commission could, with the agreement of the Member States, consider what action might be taken under the CFSP.

Question No 64 by John Cushnahan (H−0879/97)

Subject: European Food Agency

Can the Commission outline the latest developments regarding the establishment of a European Food Agency?

Answer

The Commission does not envisage setting up a European Food Agency at the present time.

In fact the Commission has just started an important reorganization of its services and has divided up its responsibilities regarding the evaluation and the control of health risks in the light of legislation.

The Commission believes that before going any further it should first examine the effects of this reorganization in view of the results already obtained and still pending, particularly to decide if setting up an agency would provide the benefits which the honourable Member seems to expect.

The Commission has started an evaluation of food inspection systems in certain Member States as well as in some third countries. It will receive the results in January.

Question No 65 by Marie‐Paule Kestelijn‐Sierens (H−0881/97)

Subject: Far‐reaching implications of Decision 97/584 prohibiting the use of risk material

Article 2 of Decision 97/584(10) stipulates that the use of specified risk material “for any purpose’ shall be prohibited. Does this mean that the use of risk material is prohibited not in foodstuffs or animal feed, or in medicinal, pharmaceutical and cosmetic products, but also in industrial applications (such as the production of synthetic materials) where there is no danger to human health? Is the Commission aware of the far‐reaching implications of this drastic measure for industries that use such material in risk‐free applications? Does the Commission not consider it more appropriate to limit the prohibition on risk material to those products likely to jeopardize human health by virtue of the purposes for which they are used?

Answer

The honourable Member should be aware that specified risk material is defined in the decision as the brains, spinal cords and spleens of certain animals. The Commission has made extensive enquiries with industry representatives but has not found any example of an industrial use for these specific tissues. It does not, therefore, consider that the potential problems referred to by the honourable Member will arise.
