Question No 66 by Ulla Sandbæk (H−0883/97)

Subject: Air transport, the environment and EU rules

In June, the Roskilde county authorities granted Københavns Lufthavne A/S Roskilde authorization to increase flights of aircraft over 10 tonnes from 2, 662 to 25, 000. The authorization was granted without an environmental impact assessment being carried out and also infringes the EU Directive on the conservation of wild birds, as there are two sites covered by that directive within the six kilometre limit it specifies. The authorization permits statutory annual take‐off and landing training, which means that aircraft will not simply be flying into or out of the area but will be going up and down from very low altitudes all the time and overflying built‐up areas and sites protected by the EU wild birds directive. It should be mentioned that the airport itself is responsible for monitoring and that the agreed altitudes are therefore often not kept to. No conditions have been laid down as regards maximum noise levels. The area is already badly affected by intensive training flights using light aircraft and helicopters, not to mention general freight and passenger traffic and flights used for parachute jumping.

How does the EU rate air transport in Denmark in relation to its directives on the environment, including the wild birds directive, and what place does Copenhagen's Roskilde Airport have in plans for the future of air transport in Europe? Will this matter be included in the evidence which the press claims is being gathered for use in proceedings to be instituted against Denmark for infringement of the wild birds directive?

Answer

1. As far as Community nature conservation legislation is concerned, the Commission is aware that there are concerns about the expansion of flight operations at Roskilde Airport and the impact this may have on bird populations in two nearby special protection areas (SPAs). This is already the subject of a complaint file for which the Commission is at present assessing the evidence before contacting Denmark about the alleged negative effects on the SPAs.

Given that bird species may become habituated to certain levels of disturbance it will first be necessary, according to Community nature legislation, to establish whether this development has significant impact. The Commission does not have an infringement procedure against Denmark on the basis of the birds directive.

2. As far as the Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) Directive is concerned, it does not set an obligation to carry out an EIA for a project like the modification of Roskilde Airport which consists of an extension of a runway to 1800m (instead of 2100m or more as foreseen in the Directive).

This case can be classified as being a modification of a project (airfield) listed in annex II of the directive. Neither the current EIA Directive nor the Danish implementing legislation require an EIA for such modifications of annex II projects. The Danish legislation on land planning, however, would require an EIA even on a modification of an existing airfield if this would cause an increase of noise level with 5 dB or more. According to the calculations by the Danish authorities this magnitude of increase of noise level in the vicinity of Roskilde Airport does not happen and no EIA has therefore to be carried out.

3. As to air transport, like other airports in the Community, Roskilde Airport is open to free operation within the Community. According to relevant Community legislation, the exercise of traffic rights can, however, be subject to operational rules relating to safety, the protection of the environment and the allocation of slots. These rules should be published and they can intervene at Community, national, regional or local level. At the request of a Member State or on its own initiative the Commission will examine the application of these rules. Moreover, this legislation would allow a Member State, in this case Denmark, when serious congestion and/or environmental problems exist to limit or refuse the exercise of traffic rights, in particular when other modes of transport can provide satisfactory levels of service.

From the point of view of air transport Roskilde Airport does not have any special place as compared with other Community airports.

Question No 67 by Jaime Valdivielso de Cué (H−0884/97)

Subject: Developing the customs union with Turkey

The Commission is preparing a document on developing the customs union with Turkey. This will include the creation of new forms of compensation which will enable Turkey to export more farm produce to the Union.

Has the Commission considered the possible adverse effects of this in those Member States, such as Spain, France, Greece, Italy and Portugal, where agriculture plays a major role in the economy and a large proportion of farm produce is exported?

If so, can the Commission provide Parliament with an outline of the likely repercussions and any possible compensation measures?

Answer

The Commission"s communication of 15 July 1997, to which the honourable Member refers, is in response to an invitation by the Council and presents a series of proposals which aim to strengthen relations with Turkey on the basis of and beyond the customs union which came into force on 31 December 1995.

As regards agriculture, since the start of its relations with Turkey, the Community has opted for a progressive easing of restrictions on trade, conditional on the adoption by Turkey of the achievements of the Community in the agricultural sector. At present, the results achieved by Turkey in this domain are modest, so a more ambitious reciprocal liberalization would have to be accompanied by real efforts by Turkey to align its agricultural policy with that of the Community. This is the direction taken by the proposals in the communication of 15 July.

The Commission followed this progressive approach in the commercial agricultural agreement recently negotiated with Turkey which should come into force at the start of next year. In fact the concessions included are limited, particularly for certain products which the Community is sensitive about, such as hazelnuts and tomato concentrate. Furthermore, for the first time, this agreement also contains concessions for the export of European agricultural products to Turkey.

Question No 68 by Allan Macartney (H−0886/97)

Subject: Democracy in European elections

The Commission is keen that national minorities and all geographical regions and all political tendencies, are fairly and democratically represented in the applicant states of eastern and central Europe. Does the Commission believe that the same principles apply to the existing 15 Member States, both for the European parliamentary elections and for the Committee of the Regions?

Answer

With regard to the election of Members of the European Parliament by a uniform electoral procedure, article 138 of the Treaty lays down that the right of initiative lies with Parliament itself and not with the Commission. The Council, acting on a proposal from Parliament and after receiving Parliament's assent, must unanimously approve the appropriate provisions that it then recommends to the Member States for adoption in accordance with their respective constitutional requirements. As the House will be aware, to date no significant progress has been made in Council on this matter.

The Amsterdam Treaty, once ratified, may offer greater flexibility, in that it would allow the Council to limit itself to fixing the basic principles of a uniform electoral system, which might facilitate agreement. As the House will know, your Committee on Institutional Affairs is actively examining how best to proceed on this matter.

Members of the Committee of the Regions are appointed for a four‐year term by the Council acting unanimously on the basis of proposals from each Member State.

Question No 69 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−0888/97)

Subject: Delay in reaching a decision on complaints regarding the Thessaloniki underground railway line

In view of the fact that work on the Thessaloniki underground railway line has been held up as a result of a complaint lodged with the Commission over six months ago, what is the reason for the unjustifiable delay by the Commission's DG XV in reaching a decision on this matter with a view to resolving the current deadlock?

It is not true that the delay by the Commission in reaching a decision, together with the transfer of funds earmarked for the project under the second CPS by the Greek Government will in fact cause the project to founder, following the expiry of the deadline for its commencement?

Has the Commission not laid itself open to criticism firstly regarding the implications of its failure to reach a decision, which could cause the project to founder and, secondly, because its position could be misinterpreted as support for Greek Government moves to obstruct and undermine the project?

Answer

The Commission is fully aware of the importance of the project and of the fact that the award procedure has been temporarily halted, as requested by the Commission, in anticipation of its decision on a complaint alleging violations of Community law on public procurement. This complaint was received by the Commission in January 1997 and completed with all supporting documentation and data by March 1997, at a time when the negotiations between the winning consortium and the Greek Government still had to be finalised. The Commission is currently examining this complex case which raises important issues of Community law. This implies the review of the voluminous documentation of the project and several meetings with the two consortia involved and the Greek Government in order to elucidate a number of points of a technical and economic nature. In that respect the Commission does not agree with the honourable Member of Parliament that the time the Commission has spent in order to examine the issues raised in this case was excessive and not justified. A decision will be adopted by the Commission in the near future.

The Commission, as guardian of the treaty, has to evaluate the totality of the elements of the file and to decide on the existence or not of a breach of Community law. The Commission has not, on the other hand, any authority to comment on the advantages of building a particular project. The Commission would like to repeat the comments made earlier, namely that it is fully aware of the importance of the project and of the fact that the award procedure has been halted in anticipation of its decision on the complaint.

Question No 70 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0889/97)

Subject: Measures to protect archaeological remains in Kerameikos

Despite vigorous protests by archaeologists in Greece and other European countries, work is continuing on a pilot tunnel in the Kerameikos area of Athens, with a view to the construction of the underground railway. The result is likely to be irreparable damage to this archaeological site in Athens, which is of inestimable historical value. The construction of the station has already necessitated the destruction of over 1000 tombs, together with a communal tomb dating from the time of the plague which occurred during the Peloponnesian War.

Since the EU is providing funding for the underground railway, will the Commission take the necessary steps to change immediately the projected route thereof, avoiding the site so as to avoid endangering other important archaeological remains in Kerameikos, such as the Taurine Monument?

Answer

The excavations carried out prior to the construction of the Athens underground railway station near Kerameikos have uncovered several ancient tombs. The result of these discoveries is being examined and objects found have been maintained by the archaeological service as is usually the case for any archaeological find.

According to the information from the Greek authorities, no decision has yet been taken on the route of the underground railway tunnel near the archaeological site at Kerameikos. The owner, SA Attiko Metro, is drilling a small trial tunnel which will help to provide an accurate impression of the nature of the soil. Based on the results of the expert reports which will then be made, the archaeological Council of the Member State, which is the competent authority in this case, will take the final decision on the route of the tunnel.

Subject: Olive oil

The European Parliament's Committee on Agriculture has now adopted its report on the reform of the COM in olive oil. Subsidies of this kind would allow optimum use to be made of olive groves for the production of high quality oil, contributing substantially both to the real economy and the creation of new jobs.

In the wake of the Luxembourg Summit, every effort is being made to direct all policies, including agricultural policies, towards the creation of new jobs; does the Commission intend to adopt the criteria set out by Parliament's Committee on Agriculture?

In its proposal the Commission will take into account this factor and also the future of the olive oil sector and the optimal use of taxpayers" money.

Question No 72 by Georges Berthu (H−0893/97)

Subject: Euro banknotes – distinctive national emblems

The decision adopted by the Council of the European Monetary Institute (EMI) on 3 December 1996 to ban all distinctive national emblems on future euro banknotes raises a number of problems of form and substance: it establishes an important principle by opting for monetary union without any distinguishing signs, which, in the event of a major crisis, could make it impossible to adopt tailored solutions; the legal basis for the decision is extremely questionable in that article 105a(1) empowers the EMI to supervise only the “technical’ aspects of the preparation of banknotes; and the decision was adopted without any form of democratic discussion either in the Member States or in the European Parliament.

Would the Commission not agree that it would be advisable for the Union not to avoid such a debate? Could this not be achieved by changing the legal basis for the decision so that it may be referred to the Council? Could the Commission, for example, submit to the Council a proposal for a decision on distinctive national emblems on euro banknotes, based on article 109l(4) (third stage) of the treaty?

Answer

According to article 105a of the EC Treaty, only the ECB is entitled to authorize the issue of banknotes in the Community. Consequently, article 109f(3) of the treaty provides that the European Monetary Institute will supervise the technical preparation of banknotes.

Given that the treaty does not confer competence on other Community institutions in this area, it seems apparent that the term “technical preparation’ should be understood in the wider sense to include the conception and selection of designs.

However, the definition of the technical specifications of euro coins comes under the competence of the Council. This division of competence between the European Monetary Institute and the Council has not been contested. And so the European Council of Amsterdam, whilst welcoming the choice relating to the conception of euro coins, also noted, referring equally to euro banknotes, that these choices represent to the citizen a tangible sign of preparations for the introduction of the euro.

So as far as the Commission is concerned, there is no reason to go back over the decisions taken, nor to prepare new acts of legislation in this regard in addition to article 109l(4) of the treaty. This is all the more so because the choice of the EMI, which was made by means of a competition and public opinion polls, has been received favourably by the wider public.

Question No 73 by David Martin (H−0898/97)

Subject: BSE – Compensation for associated industries, such as cattle and beef freight contractors

Could the Commission say what compensation has been made available for associated industries, such as the cattle and beef freight contractors, badly affected by the BSE crisis, and what monies will be made available in the future?

Answer

The Community agricultural support for the BSE crisis has consisted of two packages of aid, one for ECU 850 million destined to beef producers (Regulation (EC) No 1357/96) and a second of ECU 500 million (Regulation (EC) No 2443/96) giving additional support to beef producers or for the beef and veal sector. In both cases, maximum flexibility has been given to Member States to distribute such aids in order to adapt the measures to the real needs.

Another possibility to grant support has been state aids under articles 92/93 of the EC Treaty. In the recent past the UK authorities requested the use of this type of measure to support those parts of the beef sector which have suffered most as a result of the BSE crisis. The Commission agreed on this request.

In the current situation, the only possibility to aid the cattle and beef freight contractors would be to apply such state aids, if national authorities are prepared to do so.

Question No 75 by Marianne Eriksson (H−0902/97)

Subject: Toxic contamination scandal in the tunnel under the Hallandsås (Sweden)

Large amounts of acrylamide were released during the construction of a rail tunnel under the Hallandsåsen (Halland ridge) in Sweden, and a major environmental scandal ensued.

During the debate preceding the adoption of resolution B4‐0833/97 of 23 October 1997, Commissioner de Silguy stated that the upgrading of the railway line between Malmö and Gothenburg was one of the 14 priority TEN projects, but that the tunnel itself was not mentioned in it. Does this mean that the railway line is on the list but the tunnel, which is of course part of the line, is not?

What is the EU's responsibility when projects which are part of the EU trans‐European networks, produce environmental scandals and cause suffering and economic loss to the people who should be benefiting from them?

Answer

It is correct that the development of the West Coast railway line in Sweden forms part of the Nordic Triangle multimodal corridor which is one of the fourteen major projects of the trans‐European network adopted by the European Council in Essen.

However, given the limited resources available within the general budget of the Community, funding has been concentrated on a number of specific sections of the project in order to maximize the impact of financial intervention. Eligible costs taken into account so far for calculation of Community financial support for the West Coast line do not include expenditure on Hallandsås tunnel. Regarding current projects on the West Coast line, these concern firstly studies for the preparation of new track between Helsingbord, Landskrona and Kävlinge, including the reconstruction and relocation of stations, and secondly the preliminary investigations for new track and tunnel between Varberg City and Hamra.

On the general question of the liability of the Community in cases where environmental problems arise, the Commission considers that no such liability exists where its role is one of financial support. However, it should be noted that such financial support is always contingent upon assurances from project promoters that the requirements of Community law in a number of areas, including those of the environment, have been satisfied.

Question No 76 by Alex Smith (H−0904/97)

Subject: Transport of nuclear fuels

Last month, the World Information Service on Energy (WISE) and the Citizens' Nuclear Information Centre (CNIC), jointly published a 335 page report of the International MOX (Plutonium‐Uranium Oxide Nuclear Fuel) Assessment, or I.M.A. Project, based on the work of 15 international experts, following two years of detailed collaboration.

Will the Commission study the report and its conclusions, in particular Chapter Two on Physical Protection and Chapter 7 on the Transport of Radioactive Materials (RAM)?

And will the Commission support a full environmental impact assessment study of the transport of MOX fuel to Japan from the EU plants at Sellarfield, in the UK, La Hague in France and Dessel in Belgium?

Answer

The Commission has not yet received the International MOX Assessment (IMA) report to which the honourable Member of Parliament refers. The Commission has, however, taken the necessary steps to obtain the report.

When available, the report will be examined by the Commission and submitted to the experts at the next Commission Standing Working Group on Safe Transport of Radioactive Material which will probably meet in March next year.

It must be borne in mind that physical protection aspects fall within the sole competence of individual Member States.

Concerning the transport of radioactive material, including MOX fuel, within, to or from the Community, specific regulations established by the IAEA and the international transport modal organizations are applicable. For certain modes of transport like road and railway transport Community legislation applies(11) . According to these legal instruments each shipment of packages is authorized by the competent authorities of the country of origin and by the competent authorities of all the countries through or into which it is transported.

Community legislation on environmental impact assessment only applies to projects related to specific fixed installations, sites or infrastructures.

Question No 77 by Sirkka‐Liisa Anttila (H−0905/97)

Subject: Division of the cost of agri‐environmental measures between the EU and the Member States in the context of Agenda 2000

Agenda 2000 emphasizes the importance of agri‐environmental policy in guaranteeing the sustainable development of rural areas. Under the terms of the Commission's proposal, in future Member States will be able to make direct aid payments contingent on strict compliance with agri‐environmental provisions. The Commission supports the strengthening of agri‐environmental policy by means of an increase in the relevant budget appropriations and, where necessary, higher cofinancing rates.

Agenda 2000 does not state how the cost of enhanced environmental protection in the agricultural sector and in rural areas is to be divided up between the EU and the Member States under the forthcoming financial perspective covering the period 2000 to 2006. Should the entire financing burden fall on the Member States, levels and types of development will vary substantially from area to area. For this reason, the current practice, whereby half the funding is provided by Member States and the other half by the EU, should be continued.

In Finland, agri‐environmental support measures have an important role to play in safeguarding the profitability of extensive farming. Europe can never hope to match the USA's industrial production methods, but must seek to be competitive in terms of sustainable development, quality and know‐how.

Can the Commission state how the cost of financing agri‐environmental measures over the period 2000‐2006 will be divided between the European Union and the Member States?

Answer

The importance of the agri‐environmental programmes in Finland and throughout the Communities is well recognized by the Commission. For this reason, in Agenda 2000, the Commission foresees strengthening the targeted agri‐environmental measures and increased budgetary resources for the three accompanying measures are proposed, amounting to ECU 2.8 billion.

The Commission is currently working on the detail of the new rural development proposals, including the accompanying measures. At this stage it would be premature to indicate arrangements which the Commission may propose. However, the principle of sharing the financial cost between the Community and the national (or regional) authorities will continue. At present 50 % is paid by the Community in Finland, although 75 % is paid in objective 1 regions.

In considering the way forward for the agri‐environment programmes, the Commission will draw on experience of programmes so far. In this context, the Commission commends to the honourable Member the Commission's report on the application of Regulation (EEC) No 2078/92, submitted to Parliament earlier this month.

Question No 78 by Inger Schörling (H−0907/97)

Subject: Use of hormones in fish farming

Many EU countries use the male hormone testosterone in the production of rainbow trout or salmon. In Denmark, Finland and the United Kingdom for example fry are fed the male hormone so that those fish which would have become females after this treatment become males without a vas differens. At the next stage milt from the manipulated females is mixed with hard roe to produce a new generation of fish all of which are female. This procedure is used because females have better flesh than mature males. Fishbreeders using hormones have a competitive advantage over others who allow nature to take its course.

Other methods of manipulation use heat or pressure. By subjecting the fry to extreme pressure, up to six atmospheres, up to 90 % sexless fish are obtained. Such methods are banned in Sweden under animal welfare legislation.

What is the Commission's attitude towards these fish farming methods? May Sweden ban the sale of fish bred using these methods, on the grounds of Swedish animal welfare legislation? Will the Commission require fish products to be labelled to enable consumers to choose whether to buy and eat manipulated or naturally produced fish?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the use of the hormone testosterone for zoological purposes in the fish production referred to by the honourable Member.

We would remind the honourable member that article 5 of Directive 96/22/CE adopted by the Council in April 1996 authorizes the treatment, in sea fish farming, of young fish during the first 3 months of their lives with veterinary medication which has an androgen effect with a view to sexual inversion. This treatment produces males whose sperm, which is later used for normal insemination, will give rise to a fish population which is entirely female. In some cases thermal treatment of eggs at a temperature of 26º C for 20 minutes immediately after insemination is applied in order to produce sterile young fish.

Just as for other types of animal breeding (such as bovine, ovine caprine and equine), it is important to be able to master the production cycles of fish. The determination of the sex of young animals requires that the vet has recourse to the administration of certain hormonal substances, and this occurs within a strictly zoological framework well‐defined by Community legislation. It should be noted that, in the case of sea fish farming, the fish which are finally sold for human consumption are not those which received hormones, but their offspring.

In the light of the above, the Commission does not consider it necessary to have any sort of labelling, to the extent that Directive 96/22/CE does not provide for the labelling of meat or products which come from animals which are treated with hormonal substances for therapeutic or zoological reasons in the conditions set out by the above directive, and furthermore, in this specific case the products sold for consumption are not the same animals which received the hormone testosterone.

Finally, we would remind the honourable Member that Community legislation on animal protection does not cover fish and that any legislative measure in this domain can only come in the form of national legislation.

***

Question No 79 by Charlotte Cederschiöld (H−0916/97)

Subject: Exchange of experience concerning anti‐drugs measures

The Treaty of Amsterdam increases the EU's scope for taking action in the area of justice and home affairs. Work in this area includes the stepping‐up of efforts to combat drugs‐related crime and drug abuse. Networks and other structures are now being established to facilitate the exchange of information and coordination of efforts in the field of justice.

The EU has a drugs monitoring centre, the EMCDDA, which compiles and disseminates information and statistics on the extent of drug abuse in the Member States. However, it does not have a body or network which disseminates information on good examples of rehabilitation schemes.

Is the Commission planning to ensure that information on successful examples of rehabilitating drug addicts is disseminated to, and experience exchanged between, Member States?

Answer

The Commission shares the honourable Member"s view of the need for a balanced global approach to the drugs phenomenon as established in the European Union action plan to combat drugs 1995‐1999.(12)

The Commission reminds the honourable Member of the recent adoption by the European Parliament and the Council in December 1996 of a Community action plan for the prevention of drug addiction under a public health heading(13) . Based on article 129 of the EC Treaty, this plan aims to encourage cooperation between the Member States to support their action and to promote the coordination of their policies and programmes with a view to preventing dependency.

In real terms, its implementation is founded on the cofinancing of projects which are presented mainly by transnational networks of non‐governmental organizations and local communities, and are centred around the improvement of information, education and training on the prevention of drug addiction.

One of the priorities of the programme is to improve knowledge by means of exchanging information and experience on the prevention of relapses in drug addiction, including the rehabilitation of drug addicts.

In accordance with article 5, paragraph 2 of the decision, the commitee on the prevention of drug addiction recently received a draft annual work programme for 1999 centred around the following 4 priorities: young people and new synthetic drugs, the prevention of relapses into drug addiction, particularly vulnerable groups and the improvement of practices in Europe.

So the drug addiction programme could, in the very near future, complement activities relating to the rehabilitation of drug addicts already supported by other community programmes – such as Integra – by laying the emphasis on the exchange of information and experience on the rehabilitation of drug addicts.

The Commission highlights the complementary role of the OEDT in this area. In its annual report for 1997 it details, for example, the drugs phenomenon in Europe, provides comparable information and sets out reliable objectives for the area of demand reduction.

Question No 80 by Niall Andrews (H−0919/97)

Subject: Chemical Industry Summit against synthetic drugs

Is the Commission satisfied that existing controls on the commercial sale of chemicals are sufficiently strong to combat the illegal distribution of synthetic drugs or their components; if not, will it outline what can be done at EU and international level to provide additional controls and has the Commission any plans to hold a summit between the Commission and the chemical industry to jointly fight the illegal drugs trade?

Answer

The Commission is of the opinion that the diversion of precursors into the illicit manufacture of narcotics or psychotropic substances is adequately controlled by Council Directive 92/109/EEC as to the placing of precursors on the EC Market, and by Council Regulation 3677/90 which deals with the trade between the Community and third countries.

However, the situation merits constant attention. In this context the 1997 report of the European Monitoring Centre for Drugs and Drug Addiction (EMCDDA, based in Lisbon) has emphasized once again the Commission's role in monitoring intra‐Community and external trade of chemicals which may be used in the illicit manufacture of narcotic drugs and psychotropic substances. Moreover, this specific role of the Commission is highlighted in the EU action plan 1995‐99 to fight against drugs.

With regard to new initiatives the communication from the Commission to the Council and the European Parliament on the control on new synthetic drugs COM(97)249 final, of 23.5.1997, committed the Commission to present proposals on chemical precursors of synthetic drugs.

The Commission will shortly propose to the Council and to the European Parliament an amendment to both directive and regulation, with a view to establish a surveillance mechanism whereby close cooperation is sought between authorities and operations. This cooperation would entail a voluntary system of reporting from the industry to the authorities of suspected consignments of non‐scheduled substances.

At international level, and in the framework of the next UN General Assembly special session on drugs (UNGASS), it can be envisaged to establish special surveillance lists for the non‐scheduled substances.

The Commission has already discussed with the European chemical industry (CEFIC) the measures to be taken to set out a comprehensive and effective monitoring system of the drug precursors. At present, the organization of a summit with the industry is not yet envisaged. However, the Commission is ready to plan more meetings with the involved operators at European level in order to monitor the implementation of the legislation, and especially the amendment of the abovementioned directive and regulation. In addition, the Commission is currently funding synthetic drug supply reduction projects by extended implementation of Community law on precursor control (ECU 1, 320, 000 in 1997), through training officials, organizing meetings, and developing networks and databases to underpin rapid information exchange.

Question No 81 by Juan Colino Salamanca (H−0922/97)

Subject: The situation in the rice sector

The European rice sector is facing serious structural problems because of a combination of high production levels, massive imports and substantial restrictions on subsidized exports.

Does the Commission agree?

Why is it not submitting suggestions for the sector in the context of Agenda 2000?

Answer

The Commission is fully aware of the difficult situation the Community rice sector is facing. In fact, following a good harvest and increased imports, we expect considerable surpluses.

In order to deal with this situation, the Commission proposed to the Council to limit imports from the Overseas Territories PTOM, the quantity of which causes a severe imbalance in the market place. The Council accepted this proposal, and as a result the maximum quantity of hulled rice equivalent was fixed at 160, 000 tonnes which includes rice imported directly from ACP countries.

Specific measures to limit imports were also adopted to cover the period before the installation of the new system.

Moreover, by operating a prudent export policy, the Commission encourages sales of Community rice in the internal market.

The Commission continues to observe closely the situation in this market. If the above measures prove insufficient, it will examine the possibility of making the relevant proposals to the Council and the European Parliament.

Question No 82 by Bernie Malone (H−0925/97)

Subject: Human Rights in Yemen

Following on from Parliament's resolution of 10 April on Yemen(14) which called on the EU institutions to bring strong pressure to bear on the Government of Yemen to rectify human rights abuses, could the Commission indicate what steps it has taken since then in this regard and what initiatives it is planning in the immediate future to ensure that human rights are fully respected in Yemen?

Answer

The European Community has, on 25 November 1997, signed a new cooperation agreement with Yemen. The agreement stresses that its implementation shall be based on respect of democratic principles and fundamental human rights as set out in the Universal Declaration on Human Rights. The Commission has already supported the democratization process in Yemen with three projects in connection with the general elections of 27 April 1997, which were judged by international observers to have been free and fair. At present, the Commission, in cooperation with the Yemeni Ministry of Foreign Affairs, is in the process of formulating a project for training and awareness activities in human rights aspects targeted at the Yemeni security and police forces.

Further to this, the Yemeni Government recently has taken steps to address human rights violations through the work of three committees, one of them reporting directly to the President and another chaired by the Minister of Foreign Affairs. In addition, the government of Yemen has stated that it will welcome any parliamentarian who would like to look into and discuss the human rights issue.

Question No 83 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0928/97)

Subject: Exemption of dairy products from general tax on packaging

The Danish Government has concluded a compromise budget for final adoption this month. It includes a new general tax on packaging which exempts dairy products, among others.

Does not the Commission consider that this exemption is an act of discrimination in favour of dairy products as other products such as juices, for example, are not exempted?

Is an exemption of this kind for dairy products compatible with articles 92 and 95 of the treaty?

Answer

Community legislation, and more specifically, Directive 92/12/CEE on the general system and the holding, circulation and control of products subject to excise, leaves the Member States free to introduce or retain national, non‐harmonized taxes in addition to harmonized excise on condition that the terms of these taxes are compatible with primary and secondary Community fiscal law. So Denmark, a priori, is not breaching Community law by introducing a general tax on packaging.

Furthermore, according to Court of Justice precedent relating to article 95 of the EC Treaty, each Member State is free to set up the tax system which it considers most appropriate for each product, as long as this does not result in a situation where a product from another Member State is taxed more heavily than similar national products.

As regards the general tax on packaging in Denmark, the information provided by the honourable Member does not lead us to believe that this condition has not been met.

However, without fuller information on the nature of the measure – with reference to the reason for dairy products being exempted from it – the Commission is not in a position to judge whether or not the measure is compatible with article 92 of the EC Treaty.

***

Question No 84 by Christine Oddy (H−0931/97)

Subject: Street children in Honduras and Guatemala

Is the Commission aware of the latest publication by Casa Alianza on the torture of the street children in Guatemala and Honduras?

What is the current state of play with the Commission's programmes in Guatemala and Honduras?

Answer

The Commission has been closely following the situation of street children in Guatemala and Honduras, the principal victims of human rights abuses in those countries.

The street children's situation is the result of complex economic, social and political circumstances such as massive emigration toward the capital city, the disintegration of the family unit, the loss of cultural identity, etc.

As regards Guatemala, the European Commission adopted, in 1994, a programme of ECU 2.5 milion in favour of the street children, which was signed by the Procuraduría de los Derechos Humanos on 4 June 1995. This project has been fully operational since the beginning of 1996 and has produced very good results.

With respect to Honduras, the European Commission adopted in 1996, a programme of ECU 2.55 million in favour of the street children, which was signed by the Junta Nacional de Bienestar Social . This project will start its activities at the beginning of 1998.

Both projects are designed to increase the capacity of NGOs and public institutions to deal with the problem; the capacity of intervention of the various public authorities in charge of minors' protection (Secretaría de Bienestar Social , minors' courts, Procuraduría de los Derechos Humanos ) and to train the operatives involved in the new legislation on child protection.

Following the results of the first programme in Guatemala, the Commission is convinced that these actions represent an important step towards a solution to the problem of street children in both countries.

***

Question No 85 by Liam Hyland (H−0934/97)

Subject: Zebra Mussels

In the light of the infestation of Irish and other European rivers and waterways by the species known as the zebra mussel and of the known damage this causes to eco‐systems, hydro‐electric facilities, etc., is the Commission willing to allocate research funds to this matter so that the progress of the species can be monitored and controlled?

Answer

Due to the widespread invasion of all types (aquatic, terrestrial and coastal marine) of ecosystems by the unintended or intended introduction of non‐indigenous (or ‚exotic’) species, the question addressed by the honourable Member of Parliament regarding the ‚infestation of Irish and other European rivers and waterways by the... zebra mussel’ has to be considered as part of a wider environmental phenomenon.

This has been recognized by the Commission. Within the Specific Environment Research Programme (1990‐1994)(15) the subject of introduced species was included among the research priorities. However, on the call for research proposals, the Commission did not receive any project proposal which, on the basis of the evaluation results, could be proposed for funding.

The Community Agriculture and Fisheries Research Programme(16) funded research on the impact of introduced marine species on consumer‐relevant indigenous fish and shellfish population.

One possible option for financing projects to monitor this introduced species could be the funding, through the LifeNature programme, of a conservation project, under the habitat or bird directives, for a species threatened by the proliferation of the zebra mussel.

***

Question No 86 by Pat Gallagher (H−0935/97)

Subject: Inspection of fishing vessels in the Irish Box

Can the Commission indicate the steps being taken to address the problem of discards, and can it indicate if there are any proposals to require fishing vessels visiting ports within the Irish Box to have their vessels inspected on arrival and again on departure from the Irish Box before proceeding to other European ports, including the Canary Islands?

Answer

The regulation laying down certain technical measures for the conservation of fishery resources which was agreed upon by the Council on 30 October 1997 contains several provisions intended to reduce discards of by‐catches of quota species and juveniles. Several of these provisions were carried over from the existing regulation, while other provisions are new.

As regards monitoring of fishing vessels entering and leaving the Irish Box, the Council has adopted legislation(17) concerning continuous satellite‐based monitoring of certain categories of Community fishing vessels. Satellitetracking devices installed on board fishing vessels will ensure that position reports and, where appropriate in western waters(18) , catch data are transmitted to fisheries monitoring centres of the flag Member State and, where appropriate, coastal Member States. Definition of checkpoints at sea are therefore no longer relevant since the authorities can track the relevant fishing vessels continuously whilst operating in their waters. Inspectors will thus be in a position to inspect in due time whether fishermen abide by the rules. With respect to vessels landing in the Canary Islands, the Spanish control authorities are responsible for control and inspection. Fisheries inspectors are permanently based in Las Palmas, the only port that can handle these vessels in the Canary Islands, in order to carry out inspection duties on all landings by Community fishing vessels.

***

Question No 87 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0936/97)

Subject: Rules of origin

In view of the complexity of current legislation concerning the issue of certificates of origin, the widespread confusion caused by the issue of these certificates and the problems arising from the high financial risk for European importers, does the Commission not consider that the customs code should be amended to make it easier for European and third country importers to obtain certificates of origin in order to prevent confusing situations, which can entail high financial costs for businessmen?

Answer

1. All merchandise from third countries which benefit from preferential tariffs is accompanied by a place of origin certificate. This certificate is provided for by a document which defines the concept of “original product’ and the methods of administrative cooperation. This text is generally known as one of the “rules of origin’ and appears in the form of an agreement in the appendix of free trade agreements signed by the Community, or in clauses on application of the Community customs regulations for preferential tariffs granted independently by the Community.

2. The Commission gave the impetus for the Community"s launch some years ago of an action to renew, unify and simplify the rules of preferential origin following the increase since the start of the 1990s in the number of conventional preferential schemes and owing to the need to promote legibility and transparency in this regulation.

The preferential origin rules currently applied by the Community in Europe (EEA, EFTA, CEECs: more than thirty countries) have been identical since 1 July 1997. The same text has been proposed to the Mediterranean countries. Some of them have already accepted it.

This action to standardize and improve the preferential origin rules is based among other things on simplification of documents (elimination of EUR.2 and APR. 1 certificates, general application of invoicing statements), general application of a model text which has been largely standardized (for example, basic definitions such as the definition of “wholly obtained’ products, etc.) or even the promotion of a new type of relationship with the economic operators (general application of the concept of “approved exporters’).

Otherwise, and as far as possible, some of these new rules have been transposed both to the ACP agreement and to independent regulations applying to products from the overseas countries and territories, the republics of former Yugoslavia, or countries which benefit from the system of generalized preferences.

3. Moreover, the whole problem of the functioning of preferential tariff schemes, and therefore the problem of certificates of origin, was part of a detailed study by the Commission in the first semester of 1997. A communication relating to this(19) was sent to the Council and the European Parliament. This debate should be opened very soon within these institutions.

***

Question No 88 by Richard Howitt (H−0938/97)

Subject: Human rights in Venezuelan prisons

The Commission, at the request of the Venezuelan government, contributes to improving prison conditions, e.g. through education programmes and workshops with both prisoners and guards. Yet, effective improvements remain to be seen. In 1997 alone there have been two major incidents leading to the killing of 29 inmates in a prison in Bolivar and 16 in a prison in Maracaibo. During police and army operations children are random targets of extreme violence by members of the security forces. The UN Special Rapporteur, Mr Nigel S. Rodley, recommended that children deprived of liberty should be held exclusively in separate institutions. So far, in spite of European aid and Venezuelan government assurances on improving prison conditions and stopping human rights violations in prisons and detention centres, especially to protect children, this has not been made effective. As recommendations of NGOs and the UN rapporteur have not been met, how can the Commission justify its corresponding programmes? How may the EU expect to contribute to improving the situation of children in detention centres and police stations, with what appears to be the Venezuelan government's leniency in giving effect to its responsibilities?

Answer

There are several reasons justifying the support programme for the reintegration of delinquents and minors:

‐ first, its principal beneficiaries are prison inmates and young offenders whose conditions of detention, e.g. sanitation, hygiene, will be improved and who will be given the opportunitiy of earning an income;

‐ second, the EC has gained valuable experience in supporting the improvement of conditions of detention, through the project called the Venezuelan prison system aid programme which was praised, in particular, by a delegation of European parliamentarians who visited the country in May 1997;

‐ third, support for the structural reforms that the Government of Venezuela is carrying out, and in particular decentralization, a process through which responsibility for the prisons is transferred to the regional authorities, and finally the formal written commitment of the Venezuelan authorities to complete the construction of Barquisimeto prison. This will help reduce the overcrowding that is one of the main causes of the appalling situation in the Venezuelan detention centres.

Henceforth, the improvement of the living conditions in the prisons will de facto have a positive impact on the children's living conditions since they are often confined with their mothers. Similarly, the training activities introduced in the detention centres for young offenders will facilitate their reintegration into society.

***

Question No 89 by Enrico Ferri (H−0939/97)

Subject: The maintenance of duty‐free sales

Maintaining duty‐free sales is not legally incompatible with the rules of the single market because it is in the interests of the shared and non‐individual sovereignty of the Member States to provide economic advantages and incentives to encourage tourism and employment and safeguard consumers. Airports and ports constitute free shared areas not only for intra‐Community but also intercontinental contacts. In view of this, does the Commission intend to propose to the Council that the decision of 1991 should be revised and duty‐free sales be maintained or, secondarily and temporarily, that such sales should be continued for the purposes of useful experimentation using the single currency?

Answer

The Commission has already had several opportunities to express its view on the issue of duty‐free sales, in particular before this institution. The Commission"s position is clear, firm and well‐known.

In the single market duty‐free sales constitute a distortion of free competition between means of transport and operators.

It is a form of non‐transparent support to a specific economic sector, which leads to a transfer of resources from taxpayers as a whole to those who benefit from the duty‐free system.

So it goes without saying that the Commission has no intention of proposing to the Council any change in the decision, taken in 1991, to abolish the duty‐free system as from 30 June 1999.

***

Question No 90 by Anne McIntosh (H−0816/97)

Subject: Asylum provisions

Has the European Commission considered the fact that bogus economic asylum seekers can now shop around for the best available support package, burdening those Member States with the most generous benefit provisions?

Is this really the scenario intended by changes in European law?

Answer

With the entry into force of the Dublin Convention on 7 September 1997, there are now clear rules for determining the Member State responsible for examining an asylum request.

The Commission recalls that this convention was negotiated and signed before the entry into force of the Treaty on European Union and is therefore intergovernmental in nature. Nevertheless, the Commission, which was associated with the preparation of this convention, welcomes its entry into force. It should provide much needed certainty both for asylum applicants and for Member States. It is designed to ensure that applicants are not referred successively from one Member State to another without any of these States acknowledging itself to be competent to examine the application for asylum.

The convention has been in force for just three months, and it will of course take a little while for the new arrangements to settle down. The convention does, however, provide a mechanism for Member States to submit proposals for the revision of the convention to eliminate difficulties which arise in relation to its application or to bring any difficulties they experience to the attention of the Article 18 Committee, which is responsible for considering the application and interpretation of the convention.
